              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2014">r-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=21st-c-proposed">21st-c-proposed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer854252995" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8937941927811938451">How long do you think it would have taken Europe to move past the Middle Ages had there been no crusades or colonialism or any other garroting movement of one culture extending into another and taking back what it sees fit? What explains the disparity between the defeat of Germany and the crushing of Biafra beyond the matters of infrastructure and economic needs of cosmopolitan borders? Why is it that I have childhood memories of eat up, eat up, the children in Africa are starving, and it is onl</span>
  <span id="freeText8937941927811938451" style="display:none">How long do you think it would have taken Europe to move past the Middle Ages had there been no crusades or colonialism or any other garroting movement of one culture extending into another and taking back what it sees fit? What explains the disparity between the defeat of Germany and the crushing of Biafra beyond the matters of infrastructure and economic needs of cosmopolitan borders? Why is it that I have childhood memories of eat up, eat up, the children in Africa are starving, and it is only now that I discover the reality behind it? You say it was my fault that I didn't educate myself enough beyond the white-washed gilt of my blowhard Americanisms of the Millennial world; I ask what the fuck was all my schooling and 'respectable upbringing' for, if that wasn't a guarantee. There is a reason for the swamp of World War II pathos in entertainment, for the sniffing at "affirmative action" and "academics", for the pretenses of post-racism post-feminism post-"quality literature", and it's not me. <br /><br />I am a sucker for meaning and prettied up prose, I will grant you that. What I will refuse you is the promise that this work is more of the same. Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie is a gift, <i>a gift</i>, and she more than deserves the Nobel Prize for Literature. I'd be angrier about the old white men ubiquity of that shiny shiny shit for credibility if I thought it deserved her.<br /><br />Everyone knows the race question, the gender question, the power wielded through physiognomy and character definition, but few know how to deal with it. Fewer are those who can incorporate all that into a narrative that acknowledges the pain without focusing the guilt. If you get your hackles up over Adichie's portrayal of any of the humans here, make sure you take the time to reflect on the hows and wherefores, for unless you have a connection to Biafra lined with blood and guts instead of photographs and TIME magazine, she didn't write it for you. What a wonder, then, that this work has gotten the recognition it deserves. Almost a sign of hope for the myriad of worlds that are being continually passed over for the same old facile bigotry, but hey. Let's not get comfortable here.<blockquote>
  <i>'The war isn't my story to tell, really.'<br />Ugwu nodded. He had never thought that it was.</i>
</blockquote>Rare is the work which shows every character's flaws in an ugly world without ever declaring its citizens the same. Rare is the author who embodies a fundamentally different persona without extenuating bitterness, removing the question of 'objectivity' and all its lying obfuscation from the table completely in her effort to tell a story of her heritage. There is an evil in these pages of hers that she refuses to separate out into a single entity, a lazy route that denies the reality of succulent coercion and silent conformation and relegates our lives along the lines of political ignorance and feel good charity. You are as compromised as I, and if you think I point this out due to vindictive holier-than-thou, forget it. My incentives are helplessness and rage, my methods are writing and contextualization, and if recent misogynistic extremism fuels my efforts more than Adichie's prose, well. Knowing my audience, you'll pay attention regardless.<blockquote>
  <i>Ugwu thanked him and shook his head and realized that he would never be able to capture that child on paper, never be able to describe well enough the fear that dulled the eyes of mothers in the refugee camp when the bomber planes charged out of the sky. He would never be able to depict the very bleakness of bombing hungry people. But he tried, and the more he wrote, the less he dreamed.</i>
</blockquote>There is redemption here in all the right ways, but not for you. The bigger the gaping maw in your chest, the better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8937941927811938451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_252246552" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/252246552" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/252246552?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007200283" href="/book/show/576650.Half_of_a_Yellow_Sun">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer252246552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17376038540855362036"><i>"There is no glory" said Ugwu.</i><br/><br/>Between the covers of this book, a novel about the Biafran war and a novel about post colonial middle class life in south-eastern Nigeria awkwardly co-exist. That awkwardness, I felt, prevents the novel from being great, pushing it down into the ranks of the merely very good indeed instead.<br/><br/>The novel is split into four sections alternating between the early nineteen sixties and the later sixties. This didn't really work for me. The earlier sections don't add much to </span>
  <span id="freeText17376038540855362036" style="display:none"><i>"There is no glory" said Ugwu.</i><br /><br />Between the covers of this book, a novel about the Biafran war and a novel about post colonial middle class life in south-eastern Nigeria awkwardly co-exist. That awkwardness, I felt, prevents the novel from being great, pushing it down into the ranks of the merely very good indeed instead.<br /><br />The novel is split into four sections alternating between the early nineteen sixties and the later sixties. This didn't really work for me. The earlier sections don't add much to the later and introduce problems of their own. Flashbacks, an alternating sequence of chapters, creating two separate novels out of the whole or even beginning the novel with the violence in Kano would have been more effective in my opinion.<br /><br />Adichie manages a calm, still style that works extremely well in describing the violence, starvation and horror of the war in Biafra and give especially the later sixties sections considerable power. In particular the description of how a point of view character participates in a rape and his memory of how the young woman looks at him coupled with later finding out that something similar happened to his sister was a very powerful piece of writing.<br /><br />Unfortunately in comparison the earlier sixties sections aren't as strong. The relationship between Kainene and Richard, which the reader has to believe managed to tread the same fine line for many years, didn't convince me and the contrast between the traditional world and the metropolitan middle class characters with their international perspectives was swept away by the upheaval caused by the war.<br /><br />The conversation on the plane when a northerner freely expresses his anti-Igbo prejudices to Olanna in the mistaken belief that she was a Fulani and the brief summation of the inevitable fate of Biafra (it has oil so Nigeria will invade and African countries will not recognise it for fear of the potential for separatist movements within their own borders) effectively sum up the politics of the period from the grassroots to the international situation, but it presumes ignorance and silliness on the part of its readers <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and perhaps that is a wise move on the part of the author <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> (and that they have not read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/115476.Nostromo" title="Nostromo by Joseph Conrad" rel="nofollow noopener">Nostromo</a> ), it seems a fair bet that this is not a novel aimed at a Nigerian audience.<br /><br />I do have a slight concern as to how much is taken from the period and how much, like the threat of putting tyres round necks and setting them alight (necklacing as it was called in South Africa), the dead baby or the mention of the fate of the Herero has been sucked in by the author from other troubled situations in Africa and introduced to the 60s Nigerian milieu, but then on the other hand a novel is a work of creative fiction and only has to be believable rather than factual, or one could read this as a powerful statement of universality, Biafra as microcosm, or Africa as a collection of colonial consequences playing out extracted resources going to international markets, states cobbled together because they looked good on Imperial maps not because they had internal coherence.<br /><br />A hard novel to follow up on having dived into the deep end and come up to the surface with her arms full of war and the fate of nations.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17376038540855362036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1942410344" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1942410344" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1942410344?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1942410344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10876265283828691904">The thing about Chimamanda Adichie is, she's so appallingly good. This is the second book I've read by her and both times I'm just, like, the whole way through, I can't believe how fucking good this book is. She's perfectly positioned to be one of the great writers of our time, with her global heritage and global stories - she was born in Nigeria and continues to split her time between there and the US. She is exactly the way novels are going. And she's so good at writing them! We're watching on</span>
  <span id="freeText10876265283828691904" style="display:none">The thing about Chimamanda Adichie is, she's so appallingly good. This is the second book I've read by her and both times I'm just, like, the whole way through, I can't believe how fucking good this book is. She's perfectly positioned to be one of the great writers of our time, with her global heritage and global stories - she was born in Nigeria and continues to split her time between there and the US. She is exactly the way novels are going. And she's so good at writing them! We're watching one of the greats create herself, and that's very exciting.<br /><br />For <i>Half of a Yellow Sun,</i> her second book, she reaches back to her parents' lives, into the catastrophic Biafran War of the 60s. It's a war novel. Not at first - she spends about the first half introducing us to her characters: twin sisters Olanna and Kainene, their husbands the intellectual Odenigbo and the white guy Richard, and Olanna's houseboy Ugwu. The perspective shifts chapter by chapter between Ugwu, Olanna and Richard. All are interesting; Adichie pulls off the immense feat of making this part fully engaging, so you're not just waiting for the war. Of course she pulls it off, she's fucking balls. <br /><br />But the war does come, and you get - oh, Adichie would love this comparison - sortof a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2034413157" title="except less racist?" rel="nofollow noopener">Gone With the Wind</a> collapse from wealth to poverty. The family is Igbo - those are the people who seceded from Nigeria, fighting against the Yoruba, the other major Nigerian ethnic group, and also the Hausa. This second half is nasty stuff, so be warned: as Adichie's father would say, <i>agha ajoka.</i> War is very ugly.<br /><br />It's an actual epic, like they used to make in the olden days, ambitious and powerful. I still like Americanah just slightly better, but I wouldn't want to have to choose just one.<br /><br /><b>Appendix: Soundtrack</b><br />Music is important to Adichie - she's one of the rare writers who can really talk about music - and here the soundtrack is the Nigerian Highlife genre, a brand of Afropop. It's awesome and there's a compilation available on <a href="https://open.spotify.com/album/2gfZqgGxePS5EM9EFw2ce8" title="Spotify link" rel="nofollow noopener">Spotify</a> and <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=dMYTQA7U2o8" title="Youtube link" rel="nofollow noopener">Youtube</a>. Sound quality is absolute shit on it; <a href="https://open.spotify.com/album/4JFY8PzkeDULjFt5tevEWC" title="Spotify link" rel="nofollow noopener">Vol. 2</a> is slightly better quality, but it has less Rex Lawson and it's missing <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=A613WUWFLRg" title="Youtube link" rel="nofollow noopener">this glorious cover</a> of "Grazing in the Grass," which I only knew from <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=cjYrski71II" title="Youtube link" rel="nofollow noopener">this awesome psychedelic soul version.</a> Turns out they just made up those lyrics, who knew.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10876265283828691904" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1ky9/tcZOtHkwJdb+RB68rehFcLwl8/DoYs0ZR5lk4sVtFZq2ZLIm85vb9p4qSaPMMBjtGO+B12iNnvH6upnkA==')})
    }
  }

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_27071490'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27071490-homegoing?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Homegoing<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/14493315.Yaa_Gyasi?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Yaa Gyasi<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.45 avg rating &mdash; 178,945 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2016\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6824902764829862568\">A novel of breathtaking sweep and emotional power that traces three hundred years in Ghana and along the way also becomes a truly great American novel. Extraordinary for its exquisite language, its implacable sorrow, its soaring beauty, and for its m<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6824902764829862568\" style=\"display:none\">A novel of breathtaking sweep and emotional power that traces three hundred years in Ghana and along the way also becomes a truly great American novel. Extraordinary for its exquisite language, its implacable sorrow, its soaring beauty, and for its monumental portrait of the forces that shape families and nations, Homegoing heralds the arrival of a major new voice in contemporary fiction.\n\nTwo half-sisters, Effia and Esi, are born into different villages in eighteenth-century Ghana. Effia is married off to an Englishman and lives in comfort in the palatial rooms of Cape Coast Castle. Unbeknownst to Effia, her sister, Esi, is imprisoned beneath her in the castle\'s dungeons, sold with thousands of others into the Gold Coast\'s booming slave trade, and shipped off to America, where her children and grandchildren will be raised in slavery. One thread of Homegoing follows Effia\'s descendants through centuries of warfare in Ghana, as the Fante and Asante nations wrestle with the slave trade and British colonization. The other thread follows Esi and her children into America. From the plantations of the South to the Civil War and the Great Migration, from the coal mines of Pratt City, Alabama, to the jazz clubs and dope houses of twentieth-century Harlem, right up through the present day, Homegoing makes history visceral, and captures, with singular and stunning immediacy, how the memory of captivity came to be inscribed in the soul of a nation. \n\nGeneration after generation, Yaa Gyasi\'s magisterial first novel sets the fate of the individual against the obliterating movements of time, delivering unforgettable characters whose lives were shaped by historical forces beyond their control. Homegoing is a tremendous reading experience, not to be missed, by an astonishingly gifted young writer.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6824902764829862568\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; But after growing up together in a small, southern black community and running away at age sixteen, it\'s not just the shape of their daily lives that is different as adults, it\'s everything: their families, their communities, their racial identities. Many years later, one sister lives with her black daughter in the same southern town she once tried to escape. The other secretly passes for white, and her white husband knows nothing of her past. Still, even separated by so many miles and just as many lies, the fates of the twins remain intertwined. What will happen to the next generation, when their own daughters\' storylines intersect?\n\nWeaving together multiple strands and generations of this family, from the Deep South to California, from the 1950s to the 1990s, Brit Bennett produces a story that is at once a riveting, emotional family story and a brilliant exploration of the American history of passing. Looking well beyond issues of race, The Vanishing Half considers the lasting influence of the past as it shapes a person\'s decisions, desires, and expectations, and explores some of the multiple reasons and realms in which people sometimes feel pulled to live as something other than their origins.\n\nAs with her New York Times-bestselling debut The Mothers, Brit Bennett offers an engrossing Faced with the oppressive enthusiasms of his children, Howard feels that the first two acts of his life are over and he has no clear plans for the finale. Or the encore.\n\nThen Jerome, Howard\'s older son, falls for Victoria, the stunning daughter of the right-wing icon Monty Kipps, and the two families find themselves thrown together in a beautiful corner of America, enacting a cultural and personal war against the background of real wars that they barely register. How do you choose the work on which to spend your life? Why do you love the people you love? Do you really believe what you claim to? And what is the beautiful thing, and how far will you go to get it?\n\nSet on both sides of the Atlantic, Zadie Smith\'s third novel is a brilliant analysis of family life, the institution of marriage, intersections of the personal and political, and an honest look at people\'s deceptions.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_13588385'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13588385-there-was-a-country?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">There Was a Country: A Personal History of Biafra<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/8051.Chinua_Achebe?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Chinua Achebe<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.96 avg rating &mdash; 1,847 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2012\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer18394063505461363431\">From the legendary author of Things Fall Apart comes a longawaited memoir about coming of age with a fragile new nation, then watching it torn asunder in a tragic civil warThe defining experience of Chinua Achebe’s life was the Nigerian civil war, al<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText18394063505461363431\" style=\"display:none\">From the legendary author of Things Fall Apart comes a longawaited memoir about coming of age with a fragile new nation, then watching it torn asunder in a tragic civil warThe defining experience of Chinua Achebe’s life was the Nigerian civil war, also known as the Biafran War, of 1967–1970. The conflict was infamous for its savage impact on the Biafran people, Chinua Achebe’s people, many of whom were starved to death after the Nigerian government blockaded their borders. By then, Chinua Achebe was already a world-renowned novelist, with a young family to protect. He took the Biafran side in the conflict and served his government as a roving cultural ambassador, from which vantage he absorbed the war’s full horror. Immediately after, Achebe took refuge in an academic post in the United States, and for more than forty years he has maintained a considered silence on the events of those terrible years, addressing them only obliquely through his poetry. Now, decades in the making, comes a towering reckoning with one of modern Africa’s most fateful events, from a writer whose words and courage have left an enduring stamp on world literature.Achebe masterfully relates his experience, both as he lived it and how he has come to understand it. He begins his story with Nigeria’s birth pangs and the story of his own upbringing as a man and as a writer so that we might come to understand the country’s promise, which turned to horror when the hot winds of hatred began to stir. To read There Was a Country is to be powerfully reminded that artists have a particular obligation, especially during a time of war. All writers, Achebe argues, should be committed writers—they should speak for their history, their beliefs, and their people.Marrying history and memoir, poetry and prose, There Was a Country is a distillation of vivid firsthand observation and forty years of research and reflection. Wise, humane, and authoritative, it will stand as definitive and reinforce Achebe’s place as one of the most vital literary and moral voices of our age.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">About Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1474446019p3/11291.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11291.Chimamanda_Ngozi_Adichie">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11821003605062814716">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br/><br/>Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won t</span>
  <span id="freeText11821003605062814716" style="display:none">Chimamanda Ngozi Adichie grew up in Nigeria.<br /><br />Her work has been translated into over thirty languages and has appeared in various publications, including <i>The New Yorker</i>, <i>Granta</i>, <i>The O. Henry Prize Stories</i>, the <i>Financial Times</i>, and <i>Zoetrope</i>. She is the author of the novels <i>Purple Hibiscus</i>, which won the Commonwealth Writers’ Prize and the Hurston/Wright Legacy Award; <i>Half of a Yellow Sun</i>, which won the Orange Prize and was a National Book Critics Circle Award Finalist and a <i>New York Times</i> Notable Book; and <i>Americanah</i>, which won the National Book Critics Circle Award and was named one of <i>The New York Times</i> Top Ten Best Books of 2013. Ms. Adichie is also the author of the story collection <i>The Thing Around Your Neck</i>.<br /><br />Ms. Adichie has been invited to speak around the world. Her 2009 TED Talk, <i>The Danger of A Single Story</i>, is now one of the most-viewed TED Talks of all time. Her 2012 talk <i>We Should All Be Feminists</i> has a started a worldwide conversation about feminism, and was published as a book in 2014.<br /><br />Her most recent book, <i>Dear Ijeawele, or a Feminist Manifesto in Fifteen Suggestions</i>, was published in March 2017.<br /><br />A recipient of a MacArthur Foundation Fellowship, Ms. Adichie divides her time between the United States and Nigeria.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11821003605062814716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
